<div id="review_2612131199" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2612131199" />
    <a title="Gumble&#39;s Yard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35482263-gumble-s-yard"><img alt="Gumble&#39;s Yard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604226927p2/35482263.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2612131199?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gumble&#39;s Yard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gumble&#39;s Yard" href="/user/show/35482263-gumble-s-yard">Gumble&#39;s Yard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141188317" href="/book/show/258492.Moon_Tiger">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35482263-gumble-s-yard?shelf=2019-mookse">2019-mookse</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35482263-gumble-s-yard?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35482263-gumble-s-yard?shelf=booker-prize-winners">booker-prize-winners</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2612131199" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17347211957039188747"><blockquote>
  <i>“… crying not in grief but in wonder that nothing is ever lost, that everything can be retrieved, that a lifetime is not linear but instant. That, inside the head, everything happens at once”</i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>I came to this book as a result of its inclusion as part of the 2019 Mookse Madness tournament – but it was also an opportunity to add a Booker prize winner to the list I have read (I think this is my 22nd) and one which was generally seen as a surprise pick to represent the 1980s for the Golden Man Book</span>
  <span id="freeText17347211957039188747" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“… crying not in grief but in wonder that nothing is ever lost, that everything can be retrieved, that a lifetime is not linear but instant. That, inside the head, everything happens at once”</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />I came to this book as a result of its inclusion as part of the 2019 Mookse Madness tournament – but it was also an opportunity to add a Booker prize winner to the list I have read (I think this is my 22nd) and one which was generally seen as a surprise pick to represent the 1980s for the Golden Man Booker (surprise as it was picked ahead of Midnight’s Children – which has twice been awarded a Best of the Booker/Booker of Bookers prize I believe).<br /><br />The plot of the book is summarised here (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon_Tiger">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon_Tiger</a>) albeit that does not capture the process of memory and of different perspectives on events which drives the book – as the opening quote implies.<br /><br />Overall I thought this was an excellent book – much stronger than I was expecting. Comparisons with “English Patient” are inevitable and I think it’s a close run thing: Ondaatje’s descriptive writing and command of language gives him an early lead in my view: but Lively I think creates a more memorable and rounded character in Claudia, and is so much better at observing the nuances social interactions (a struggle for social supremacy between Claudia and Japser’s ageing ex-lothario father Sasha for instance, is brilliantly conveyed in a few lines). And this book’s ending, while inevitable, does not mar the story like “English Patient”.<br /><br />What I really enjoyed in this book was its examination of memory and history: individual memory and how perspective alters over time and varies between different individuals; the contrast and interaction between collective historical narrative and individual experiences.<br /><br />I had two main criticisms of the book: <br /><br />The relationship between Claudia and Gordon at first intriguing in its narcissistic exclusionary nature, I think veers too far when it becomes incestuous. <br /><br />The character of Laszlo seemed to me simply a device to allow Claudia’s life history to encompass not just the World Wars but the events of 1956 (Suez and the decay of British influence being an obvious one given her history in Egypt, but the link to Budapest seeming to me unnecessary).<br /><br />But overall I found this an excellent read and an unexpected delight.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17347211957039188747" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1002476988" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1002476988" />
    <a title="Paul Secor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5737008-paul-secor"><img alt="Paul Secor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464100425p2/5737008.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1002476988?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Secor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Secor" href="/user/show/5737008-paul-secor">Paul Secor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780802110275" href="/book/show/2703126-moon-tiger">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5737008-paul-secor?shelf=keepers-at-least-for-now">keepers-at-least-for-now</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5737008-paul-secor?shelf=favorite-fiction">favorite-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5737008-paul-secor?shelf=rereads">rereads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1002476988" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5565346758303905863">One of my rereads for 2020. <br/><br/>This time through, I was struck by the fact that Penelope Lively somehow managed to make Claudia Hampton a likable character (at least I felt that she was) although she considers almost every other person in her life to be beneath her - her brother and her lover being two exceptions. She basically abandons her daughter to the care of her two grandmothers, partly because she deems her uninteresting. She looks down on the father of her child because he's made a lot of m</span>
  <span id="freeText5565346758303905863" style="display:none">One of my rereads for 2020. <br /><br />This time through, I was struck by the fact that Penelope Lively somehow managed to make Claudia Hampton a likable character (at least I felt that she was) although she considers almost every other person in her life to be beneath her - her brother and her lover being two exceptions. She basically abandons her daughter to the care of her two grandmothers, partly because she deems her uninteresting. She looks down on the father of her child because he's made a lot of money creating cheesy historical spectacles for television, yet when she's presented with the same opportunity, she takes the money and runs. <br />I'm not sure how Ms. Lively managed to avoid making her thoroughly unlikable, but somehow she did. There are times when writers have a similarity to magicians.<br /><br /><br />"...eclecticism has always been my hallmark. That's what they've said, though it has been given other names. Claudia Hampton's range is ambitious, some might say imprudent: my enemies. Miss Hampton's bold conceptual sweep: my friends."<br /><br />"Mythology is much better stuff than history. It has form; logic; a message. I once thought I was a myth. Summoned to the drawing-room, aged six or so, to meet a relative richer and more worldly than Mother, of whom Mother was in awe, I found myself swept up, held at arms' length by this gorgeous scented woman, exclaimed at: 'And here she is! The little myth! A real delicious red-haired green-eyed little myth!' Upstairs, I examined my hair and eyes in the nursery mirror. I am a Myth. I am Delicious. 'That'll do, Claudia," says nurse. 'Handsome is as handsome does.' But I am a Myth. I gaze at myself in satisfaction."<br /><br />"Children are infinitely credulous. My Lisa was a dull child, but even so she came up with things that pleased and startled me. 'Are there dragons,' she asked? I said that there were not. 'Have there ever been?' I said that all the evidence was to the contrary. 'But if there is a word dragon,' she said, there must have been dragons.'"<br />I could imagine Ali Smith writing that 20 years after this book was published.<br /><br />"Of course, intelligence is always a disadvantage. Parental hearts should sink at the first signs of it. It was an immense relief to me that Lisa's was merely average. Her life has been the more comfortable. Neither her father nor I have had comfortable lives, though whether we would have wished them different is another matter. Gordon's (her brother) life has been intermittently uncomfortable, but then so, come to that, has Sylvia's (her sister-in-law), which would appear to destroy my theory about intelligence and happiness. Sylvia is profoundly stupid."<br /><br />"We open our mouths and out flow words whose ancestries we do not even know. We are walking lexicons. In a single sentence of idle chatter we preserve Latin, Anglo-Saxon, Norse; we carry a museum inside our heads, each day we commemorate peoples of whom we have never heard."<br /><br /><br />I've never had a desire to meet writers. Looking back, I think that I've only met one writer for anything more than a few minutes. However, if I could spend some time with any living writer, I believe that I would choose Penelope Lively. It would be fascinating to hear her expound on interests of hers of which I have no knowledge -archaeology, history, gardening, and whatever else she might wish to share. Strictly a fantasy, but fantasies can be interesting.<br /><br />I raised my rating to five stars after thinking more about this book and writing my review.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5565346758303905863" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1880513610" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1880513610" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1880513610?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780802110275" href="/book/show/2703126-moon-tiger">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1880513610" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14511883718318431083">An impressive account of war-correspondent and popular-historian Claudia, from her childhood to her death, the story she tells herself in a hospital bed at the end of a long life, its style mimicking the way a person might remember, without it being so-called stream-of-consciousness. Claudia’s thought processes include eras she didn’t live through—those of Pilgrims and Aztecs, for example—connecting those times to herself and to the time she did live through. <br/><br/>The narrative also gets handed off i</span>
  <span id="freeText14511883718318431083" style="display:none">An impressive account of war-correspondent and popular-historian Claudia, from her childhood to her death, the story she tells herself in a hospital bed at the end of a long life, its style mimicking the way a person might remember, without it being so-called stream-of-consciousness. Claudia’s thought processes include eras she didn’t live through—those of Pilgrims and Aztecs, for example—connecting those times to herself and to the time she did live through. <br /><br />The narrative also gets handed off in paragraphs to the important people of her life: the reader sees of them what Claudia will never know, just as the same is true of what they will never know about Claudia. <br /><br />If any of this sounds impersonal, it’s far from that: Claudia is a forceful character, living her life after WWII with verve and engagement despite a huge sadness at her core. An overarching image of memories and moments as kaleidoscopic shards of bright colors is sustained throughout and is especially brilliant at the end.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14511883718318431083" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_75448074" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75448074" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/166376-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345482402p2/166376.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/75448074?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer75448074" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18383954403457674893">"Moon Tiger", for which the author won the Booker prize, is a book that I could admire, but not like. The main protagonist, Claudia Hampton, an accomplished historian, lies dying in a London hospital bed and looks back upon her life. The resulting series of first-person flashbacks, interspersed with third-person accounts of the same episodes, coalesce into a tightly constructed kaleidoscopic view of Claudia's life which is impressive for the skill with which it is achieved, but ultimately left m</span>
  <span id="freeText18383954403457674893" style="display:none">"Moon Tiger", for which the author won the Booker prize, is a book that I could admire, but not like. The main protagonist, Claudia Hampton, an accomplished historian, lies dying in a London hospital bed and looks back upon her life. The resulting series of first-person flashbacks, interspersed with third-person accounts of the same episodes, coalesce into a tightly constructed kaleidoscopic view of Claudia's life which is impressive for the skill with which it is achieved, but ultimately left me unmoved. <br /><br />My fundamental problem with the book is that Claudia is such a self-satisfied narcissist that the reader ultimately tires of the recital of her various accomplishments and the smug superiority with which lesser characters in her history (her unfortunate sister-in-law, her disappointingly conventional daughter) are dismissed. Lively is no fool, and attempts to mitigate Claudia's unrelenting smugness with a brief episode of vulnerability and genuine emotion during a doomed World War II romance with a British tank commander who is subsequently killed in battle. The jacket cover inflates this episode by describing it as "the still point of her turning world", but the problem is that it fails to ring true. Ultimately, the version of Claudia that dominates the narrative is that of the smug, superior harpie. To whom my reaction was - why should anyone possibly care?<br /><br />So, while I can admire the skill with which this book was written, the emotional vacuum at its core ultimately leaves me cold. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18383954403457674893" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17457516193681375976">Long before <i>The English Patient</i> there was this very taut novel about WWII in north Africa.   <span id="freeText17457516193681375976" style="display:none">Long before <i>The English Patient</i> there was this very taut novel about WWII in north Africa. There isn't a wasted word in the entire novel and while you may not always sympathize with the main character (Claudia), you will enjoy her razor-sharp wit. Her daughter is portrayed as a dull product of a love affair but Lively doesn't allow that to go on either, allowing the daughter to have her say. I've read this countless times because Lively is a master or rather mistress of economy with words and yet she takes the reader through decades. This is a thinking person's romance. Nothing sappy or stupid about it. Just the genuine emotions that come from the unexpected during a wartime romance. I can't recommend it highly enough. </span>

<div id="review_3555109653" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3555109653" />
    <a title="JimZ" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/99765491-jimz"><img alt="JimZ" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575257175p2/99765491.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3555109653?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="JimZ" class="user" itemprop="url" name="JimZ" href="/user/show/99765491-jimz">JimZ</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/12892853-moon-tiger">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3555109653" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7454985039931893547">I gave this a B- in 1997. I don’t remember reading it. 🙁 I wonder what my threshold is: At what age do I remember vaguely reading a book vs. definitely reading a book vs. not reading a book when I have indeed read the book? Not reading a book when I have read it in statistics lingo would mean I committed a Type II error. I imagine I have committed some of them—I just hope the threshold is something like 15 years as opposed to one year. 😐 Reporting that I have read a book when in reality I did no</span>
  <span id="freeText7454985039931893547" style="display:none">I gave this a B- in 1997. I don’t remember reading it. 🙁 I wonder what my threshold is: At what age do I remember vaguely reading a book vs. definitely reading a book vs. not reading a book when I have indeed read the book? Not reading a book when I have read it in statistics lingo would mean I committed a Type II error. I imagine I have committed some of them—I just hope the threshold is something like 15 years as opposed to one year. 😐 Reporting that I have read a book when in reality I did not read it would be a Type I error (‘false alarm’), by the way. I don’t think I have made many of them… <br /><br />Dame Penelope Lively is 87 years old. She has published a raft of children’s fiction and adult fiction. This book won her a Booker Prize in 1987—she had been on the shortlist for two earlier works, The Road to Lichfield (1977) and According to Mark (1984).<br /><br />I would say this is definitely a good book and with very fine writing. Perhaps you should read it—take a gander at some of the reviews below. It did not resonate with me all that well because began to dislike the main protagonist, Claudia, early on in the book. So I didn’t much care what happened to her—good or bad—as she lay in her hospital bed, dying, at an old age having flashbacks and reminisces of her life, sometimes brought on by the people that were visiting her in the hospital on any given day (a current long-time lover Jasper, their daughter Lisa, her sister-in-law Sylvia). One person not visiting her died many years ago in WWII. We learn about him, Tom Southern, via her reminisces and his wartime diary. There’s a shocker in this book…I’ll encapsulate it as a spoiler. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Claudia has an incestuous relationship with her brother, Gordon (I think he is older by two years and I think the relationship occurred when they were in their 20s. I was shocked when coming upon it…like WTF? I guess I could see it—late in the book Lively takes note of it this way: “Gordon died 5 years ago. I am separate from him now. No day passes in which I do not think of him, but I can do so with detachment. He is complete; he has beginning and end. The times in which we were together are complete. I do not mourn him any the less, but I have had to move away: there is no choice. We were children together; we made narcissistic love; we grew up and depended upon one another. From time to time we loathed each other but even in hatred we were united, exclusive, a community of two. I knew Gordon as ruthlessly as I know myself–and as indulgently. What I felt for Gordon was classifiable as love for lack of a better word: he was my sense of identity, my mirror, my critic, judge and ally. Without him I was diminished.”  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />How can I care about Claudia when she showed little love towards her daughter, Lisa, growing up, tended to make the girl feel bad at times, and disliked her sister-in-law, Sylvia, for apparently the mortal sin of marrying her brother Gordon (apparently she was not as intelligent as Claudia, but then who was with her ego)? <br /><br />I’m asking myself why I am only giving this book 3 stars, because there are other books I have read in which I have rated them quite highly, in which I disliked the protagonist. I’m hard-pressed to give a coherent answer except perhaps to say I wasn’t invested with any of the characters in the book. <br /><br />Moon Tiger…as far as I know it was a mosquito repellant…one of those coils you light and it slowly emits a thin stream of something (smoke?) in the air (The Moon Tiger is a green coil that slowly burns all night, repelling mosquitos, dropping away into lengths of grey ash, its glowing red eye a companion of the hot insect-rasping darkness). <br /><br />Reviews:<br />Well this reviewer (Sam Jordison) disagrees with me to be sure: Ludicrously patronised by reviewers, Penelope Lively's novel is actually one of the very best Booker winners ever <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.theguardian.com/books/booksblog/2010/mar/19/booker-club-moon-tiger">https://www.theguardian.com/books/boo...</a><br />Another super-positive review from NPR’s You Must Read This: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.npr.org/2011/09/26/140598095/a-lively-independent-heroine-worth-mooning-over">https://www.npr.org/2011/09/26/140598...</a><br />For the author Toby Litt…<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://tobylitt.wordpress.com/2019/02/10/moon-tiger-by-penelope-lively-review/">https://tobylitt.wordpress.com/2019/0...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="7454985039931893547" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1244535338" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1244535338" />
    <a title="Melki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5293536-melki"><img alt="Melki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308071306p2/5293536.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1244535338?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melki" href="/user/show/5293536-melki">Melki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060972004" href="/book/show/2045561.Moon_Tiger">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=family-based-fiction">family-based-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1244535338" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5102288206187630980">Late life reminiscences have been done before, though they are rarely as eloquent as this one. <br/><br/>Claudia lies abed, reexamining the years and the people with whom she spent them. She recalls the complex relationship with her brother Gordon. As a child, she had once asked God to eliminate him - <i>painlessly but irreversibly</i>, but as adults, they were more like a married couple, so closely tuned it was almost incestuous. She also fondly and sadly remembers her brief affair with Tom, the only man she ev</span>
  <span id="freeText5102288206187630980" style="display:none">Late life reminiscences have been done before, though they are rarely as eloquent as this one. <br /><br />Claudia lies abed, reexamining the years and the people with whom she spent them. She recalls the complex relationship with her brother Gordon. As a child, she had once asked God to eliminate him - <i>painlessly but irreversibly</i>, but as adults, they were more like a married couple, so closely tuned it was almost incestuous. She also fondly and sadly remembers her brief affair with Tom, the only man she ever truly loved.<br /><br />Interrupting her memories are visits from Jasper, the father of her child, her painfully ordinary and boring sister-in-law Sylvia and Claudia's unremarkable daughter Lisa. <br /> <br /> <br />As she did in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/202858.The_Photograph" title="The Photograph by Penelope Lively" rel="nofollow noopener">The Photograph</a>, Lively takes haughty, somewhat unlikable characters and transforms them into fascinating people. Here is a beautifully written book, well deserving of its prize. I absolutely loved this bit where an elderly and hospitalized Claudia muses about language:<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Today language abandoned me. I could not find the word for a simple object - a commonplace familiar furnishing. For an instant, I stared into a void. Language tethers us to the world; without it we spin like atoms. Later, I made an inventory of the room - a naming of parts: bed, chair, table, picture, vase, cupboard, window, curtain. Curtain. I breathed again. <br />We open our mouths and out flow words whose ancestries we do not even know. We are walking lexicons. In a single sentence of idle chatter we preserve Latin, Anglo-Saxon, Norse; we carry a museum inside our heads, each day we commemorate peoples of whom we have never heard. More than that, we speak volumes - our language is the language of everything we have not read. Shakespeare and the Authorised Version surface in supermarkets, on buses, chatter on radio and television. I find this miraculous. I never cease to wonder at it. That words are more durable than anything, that they blow with the wind, hibernate and reawaken, shelter parasitic on the most unlikely hosts, survive and survive and survive.</i>
</b><br /> <br /> As Lively proves, looking back on loves lost, opportunities missed, deeds done and regrettable words exchanged can be both a luxury and a curse.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5102288206187630980" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2583267433" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2583267433" />
    <a title="Betsy Robinson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5149179-betsy-robinson"><img alt="Betsy Robinson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404493492p2/5149179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2583267433?book_show_action=true">Nov 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Betsy Robinson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Betsy Robinson" href="/user/show/5149179-betsy-robinson">Betsy Robinson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2583267433" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer929031220331682788"><b><i>Moon Tiger</i> by Penelope Lively</b><br/><br/><blockquote>. . . everything can be retrieved, . . . a lifetime is not linear but instant. . . . inside the head, everything happens at once. (68)</blockquote>That quote describes both the plot and structure of this stunning book.<br/><br/>Claudia Hampton is 76 and dying, and as she dies, she tells the story of her world—all its failures, successes, loves (forbidden and lost), peoples, times, and points of view. She jumps from first person to third to different first persons, and it is not the least b</span>
  <span id="freeText929031220331682788" style="display:none"><b><i>Moon Tiger</i> by Penelope Lively</b><br /><br /><blockquote>. . . everything can be retrieved, . . . a lifetime is not linear but instant. . . . inside the head, everything happens at once. (68)</blockquote>That quote describes both the plot and structure of this stunning book.<br /><br />Claudia Hampton is 76 and dying, and as she dies, she tells the story of her world—all its failures, successes, loves (forbidden and lost), peoples, times, and points of view. She jumps from first person to third to different first persons, and it is not the least bit confusing.<br /><br />I suspect that many of us with decades behind us can relate to this way of recollecting. A color, a sound, anything can set off a memory as real now as it was in the time that it happened. And likewise, within the memory, we may jump to still other associations. I imagine this is the normal way we review our lives: as a kind of jumble of jigsaw pieces that only we understand as a whole picture. And Penelope Lively wrote it, and it is not only comprehensible but moving.<br /><br />What is particularly moving to me is our impermanence: we’re here, then we’re not; events that feel so compelling, all-consuming, and even historic happen, and then they’re gone and only remain in memory until everybody who holds the memory is gone; places are here, then replaced by others or transformed into unrecognizable new ones. This is sad, but it is also a comforting perspective on my life and current times.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="929031220331682788" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_260187868" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/260187868" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/260187868?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060972004" href="/book/show/2045561.Moon_Tiger">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=500-wm">500-wm</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand">1-read-on-hand</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=3-star">3-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2015">r-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-read">antidote-think-twice-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-all">antidote-think-twice-all</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-check">reality-check</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer260187868" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer647356107657751328">I've been musing on feminism and the prevalence of white supremacism in its most visible structure which no. Nope. Uh-uh. So, what does that leave me in terms of the where-do-we-go-from-here? Strange and terrifying and unthinkable places, judging from conversations both online and off, because if social justice is less realization and more Inception and a much deeper plain is called for than what is sustained by the majority of entertainment both canonical and otherwise, what are we allowed to a</span>
  <span id="freeText647356107657751328" style="display:none">I've been musing on feminism and the prevalence of white supremacism in its most visible structure which no. Nope. Uh-uh. So, what does that leave me in terms of the where-do-we-go-from-here? Strange and terrifying and unthinkable places, judging from conversations both online and off, because if social justice is less realization and more Inception and a much deeper plain is called for than what is sustained by the majority of entertainment both canonical and otherwise, what are we allowed to appreciate?<br /><br />I have no answers for the average reader of what I write. All I know is this work stayed on my shelves till it resembled <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19528.The_Mandarins" title="The Mandarins by Simone de Beauvoir" rel="nofollow noopener">The Mandarins</a> rather than the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/347413.Sleepless_Nights" title="Sleepless Nights by Elizabeth Hardwick" rel="nofollow noopener">Sleepless Nights</a> I was hoping for. White women going around with their lovers and their daughters and their wars and politics and frustrated dreams, and yes their pain is recognizable but does it have to be worked out on the backs of colonialism-strengthening-tourism and the mournful histories of those whose descendants are pretended extinct by the common pen? Don't get me wrong, white women can help if they really try in the form of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24100.The_Golden_Notebook" title="The Golden Notebook by Doris Lessing" rel="nofollow noopener">The Golden Notebook</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/526927.Burger_s_Daughter" title="Burger's Daughter by Nadine Gordimer" rel="nofollow noopener">Burger's Daughter</a>, and of course many do not believe the quality of literature relies on how much it can "help", so the choice has ever and always been yours.<br /><br />The movie based on the main character's book about the history of Moctezuma II and Hernán Cortés smacks of today's <i>Exodus: Gods and Kings</i>, banned in the Egypt that does not come alive in this book beyond slums and walkers on Pyramids and the plot lines of white people dehumanizing everything for their own, what? Centering in the universe? Because let me tell you, it's a sad and lonely existence that believes being on top is worth the sacrifice of taking into serious consideration everything else. Claudia had enough money and beauty and sentimental savior complex to last her to the very end, which is more than can be said for most everyone else. Sure, she held her own in a white male dominated world, but a world beyond European borders does not exist for the sole purpose of personal development.<br /><br />It's clever and witty and hints at the deeper things, I'll give it that. Ultimately, however, it encourages a hollowness I can no longer afford.</span>
  <a data-text-id="647356107657751328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_836360978" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/836360978" />
    <a title="Jonathan Pool" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27950748-jonathan-pool"><img alt="Jonathan Pool" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484567350p2/27950748.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/836360978?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan Pool" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan Pool" href="/user/show/27950748-jonathan-pool">Jonathan Pool</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/12892853-moon-tiger">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27950748-jonathan-pool?shelf=booker-listed">booker-listed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27950748-jonathan-pool?shelf=british_irish-contemporary">british_irish-contemporary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer836360978" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17394830185636798966">Moon Tiger has been the focus of much, renewed, attention since its surprise selection as a Booker 50 decade winner (for 1987- selected by Lemn Sissay).<br/>Penelope Lively has always had her supporters and Moon Tiger is a work of some style and substance that has not dated.<br/>I heard Penelope Lively speak at the Booker 50 and it was a true pleasure to witness her strength, her independence, her lucidity at age eighty five.<br/>It is tempting to ask whether the Claudia Hampton of Moon Tiger is semi autobiogr</span>
  <span id="freeText17394830185636798966" style="display:none">Moon Tiger has been the focus of much, renewed, attention since its surprise selection as a Booker 50 decade winner (for 1987- selected by Lemn Sissay).<br />Penelope Lively has always had her supporters and Moon Tiger is a work of some style and substance that has not dated.<br />I heard Penelope Lively speak at the Booker 50 and it was a true pleasure to witness her strength, her independence, her lucidity at age eighty five.<br />It is tempting to ask whether the Claudia Hampton of Moon Tiger is semi autobiographical. The Egypt setting matches Lively’s own youth. The question of whether first person, narrator - led, fiction is veiled autobiography is a charge often made by interviewers. At The Royal Festival Hall for Booker 50 (July 2018), Lively raised the subject herself and said she had mixed feelings about Claudia, who she said was, emphatically, not her!!<br />The renewed plaudits for Moon Tiger concur that this is a book that has found its time now. It has staying power, it has a life of its own.<br />Lively talked about the inspiration behind it. Her own memories growing up in Cairo, coupled with hours of research spent in the Imperial War Museum archives.<br />It’s a book about the nature of evidence, and of memory.<br /><br />I enjoyed the book, the writing style, very much.<br />The backdrop of war, (Egypt 1940-4), at close quarters, invariably brings an intensity to a love affair; an untrammelled passion, that’s quite unlike the love that develops in ‘normal’ courtship. There’s no time to waste, the abandonment of worry to stay in the moment of love is intoxicating.<br />The similarities of Moon Tiger and <b> The English Patient </b>(the winner of Booker 50) in this respect are obvious. Luxor- “a world of dust and water” (75)<br />Claudia Hampson’s meeting of Tom Southern is intense, conducted at accelerated speed, and you just know it’s going to end up realistically, tragically. The war generation would not have had the inclination to stress about the complicated, convoluted shenanigans that typify today’s marriages, affairs, jealousies.<br />Claudia Hampton is unapologetic, ostensibly cantankerous, as she lies in her hospital bed. She’s not actually difficult or unreasonable, just a woman whose life experiences (and Lively throws in a number of non - war hardships) are such that faced with a cancerous tumour she doesn’t intend to bow out meekly.<br /><br />“If feminism had been around then I’d have taken it up, I suppose;As it was I never felt it’s absence, being a woman seemed to me a valuable extra asset. My gender was never an impediment”(14)? <br /><br />Lively’s characters are not generally nice. Claudia’s ex husband, Jasper is a narcissist, her brother is sly,”Gordon’s intellectual energy is prodigious; his emotional energy is minimal“(24). His wife,Sylvia, is an invisible presence “she has given little trouble. She has devoted herself to children and houses”(23). Families.<br />Penelope Lively punctuates her depictions of people with waspish stings.<br />I was reminded at times of Elizabeth Strout’s <b>My Name is Lucy Barton </b>(2016), also a bedside reveal of family dysfunction. Claudia’s own daughter is an overlooked footnote: “My Lisa was a dull child”(9),<br />Moon Tiger’s “history of the world” reveals itself in a series of flashbacks; the non linear structure is challenging at first, and it’s a book absolutely made for a second, even more fulfilling read.<br />The final third draws back from the excitement, the life and vitality of our narrator’s glorious, albeit brief, years. It’s a facsimile of all our lives that while the end is known (death), we don’t always know when our peak time has arrived.<br />A fine book, and one indeed that has found its place.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17394830185636798966" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer318642147" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer7371357016691803542">This book positively shimmered. I thought about it for days afterward, and not for any specific reason apart from sheer awe at this author's skill. This novel is perhaps the best book I've read all year. Her economy of phrase, wit, and ability to apply a dream-like sheen to a whole compendium of characters makes this book a strange journey, much like an odd dream that you wake up wondering, "was that real?" <br/><br/></span>

<div id="review_113689806" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/113689806" />
    <a title="Moses Kilolo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2423887-moses-kilolo"><img alt="Moses Kilolo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516734892p2/2423887.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/113689806?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Moses Kilolo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Moses Kilolo" href="/user/show/2423887-moses-kilolo">Moses Kilolo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060972004" href="/book/show/2045561.Moon_Tiger">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer113689806" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6632238042006110915">The depth of this novel is amazing. Even though there are numerous points of view, and the main narrator does not follow a chronological account, every page of the book is a small piece of lovely reading pleasure. <br/><br/>This is not to say it is a quick read, sometimes some sections are so deep one wants to read them over again. The wisdom of looking into the motivations of mankind, their power to decide and suffer the consequence of those decisions is what Lively tackle’s in Moon Tiger. Caudia, the ma</span>
  <span id="freeText6632238042006110915" style="display:none">The depth of this novel is amazing. Even though there are numerous points of view, and the main narrator does not follow a chronological account, every page of the book is a small piece of lovely reading pleasure. <br /><br />This is not to say it is a quick read, sometimes some sections are so deep one wants to read them over again. The wisdom of looking into the motivations of mankind, their power to decide and suffer the consequence of those decisions is what Lively tackle’s in Moon Tiger. Caudia, the main character, is writing a book towards the end of her life and calls it the history of the world. <br /><br />But then it is her own personal history, mirroring that of her world and the way in which that world affected her story in it.<br /><br />Aged and hospitalized, Claudia revisits the life that she has lived and the characters that have been of consequence to that life. As expected these are the people that she has been closest to; family, lovers, and some striking acquaintances. She remembers with astonishing depth the joys as well as troubles she has had with men, her struggle to be a good mother to her daughter Lisa, and the more than unnatural friendship and intimacy with her brother Gordon for whom she speaks very highly;<br /><br /><blockquote>:he was my sense of identity, my mirror, my critic, judge and ally. Without him I am diminished.</blockquote><br />Claudia’s history of the world is replete with incidences that have shaped her own world. She admits that one’s history, even though they do live in the same world and in the same time frame, may not always resemble the history of another. We are all unique. And it is our mental and spiritual experience that constitutes that difference.<br /><br />The novel is most admirable for its intricate attention to detail. Its characters have been so well observed that one feels a tinge of familiarity, as though these are people one has somehow always known. Sometimes even becoming a mirror for the absurdities in the readers own life, or even their struggle toward love, a sense of belonging. <br /><br />Lively was born in Cairo, and it is her childhood memory for which she revisits to provide a very realistic setting for her novel. The time of war, which covers a significant section towards the end of the novel, is a time when her history of the world is best remembered. But you imagine the loss, the deaths, the memories, and it breaks your heart.<br /><br />And coming with such fine narrative, this novel is worth reading and rereading.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6632238042006110915" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('POQOklflRb5pxPhrLKErc0IRvJGnMQFmgWseOH6/1AEGwmdA7LuM1s7vTArbVCGslE5bWWm8jwHaFViJs/wmmw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UxCu3gY3bGnKt3w3tmTSPRCUnCArbTs3SzYdxW61KcBpNscMvWmlAW2cyFZBkdjixst76OXgtVAQSFt0o/bbWg==')})
  }

Two English people--Bobby, a civil servant with a guilty appetite for African boys, and Linda, a supercilious compound wife -- are driving back to their enclave after a stay in the capital. Filled with the &quot;sacred hunger&quot; to expand its empire and its profits, England entered full into the slave trade and spread the trade throughout its colonies. In this Booker Prize-winning work, Barry Unsworth follows the failing fortunes of William Kemp, a merchant pinning his last chance to a slave ship; his son who needs a fortune because he is in love with an upper-class woman; and his nephew who sails on the ship as its doctor because he has lost all he has loved. extraordinary counterpoint of passions and ideas.\n\nMan Booker Prize Winner (1990)<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"14492209842256698085\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/9738.Penelope_Lively">About Penelope Lively</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/9738.Penelope_Lively"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1224211733p3/9738.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/9738.Penelope_Lively">Penelope Lively
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
663
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6739027953567259435">Penelope Lively is the author of many prize-winning novels and short-story collections for both adults and children. She has twice been shortlisted for the Booker Prize: once in 1977 for her first novel, <i>The Road to Lichfield</i>, and again in 1984 for <i>According to Mark</i>. She later won the 1987 Booker Prize for her highly acclaimed novel <i>Moon Tiger</i>.<br/><br/>Her other books include <i>Going Back</i>; <i>Judgement Day</i>; <i>Nex</i></span>
  <span id="freeText6739027953567259435" style="display:none">Penelope Lively is the author of many prize-winning novels and short-story collections for both adults and children. She has twice been shortlisted for the Booker Prize: once in 1977 for her first novel, <i>The Road to Lichfield</i>, and again in 1984 for <i>According to Mark</i>. She later won the 1987 Booker Prize for her highly acclaimed novel <i>Moon Tiger</i>.<br /><br />Her other books include <i>Going Back</i>; <i>Judgement Day</i>; <i>Next to Nature, Art</i>; <i>Perfect Happiness</i>; <i>Passing On</i>; <i>City of the Mind</i>; <i>Cleopatra’s Sister</i>; <i>Heat Wave</i>; <i>Beyond the Blue Mountains</i>, a collection of short stories; <i>Oleander, Jacaranda</i>, a memoir of her childhood days in Egypt; <i>Spiderweb</i>; her autobiographical work, <i>A House Unlocked</i>; <i>The Photograph</i>; <i>Making It Up</i>; <i>Consequences</i>; <i>Family Album</i>, which was shortlisted for the 2009 Costa Novel Award, and <i>How It All Began</i>.<br /><br />She is a popular writer for children and has won both the Carnegie Medal and the Whitbread Award. She was appointed CBE in the 2001 New Year’s Honours List, and DBE in 2012.<br /><br />Penelope Lively lives in London. She was married to Jack Lively, who died in 1998.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6739027953567259435" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/trivia/work/2877263-moon-tiger">Trivia About Moon Tiger</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
        <div class="mediumText">
          6 trivia questions
          <br />
          
        </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right;" href="/trivia/work/2877263-moon-tiger">More quizzes &amp; trivia...</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/2877263-moon-tiger">Quotes from Moon Tiger</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Language tethers us to the world; without it we spin like atoms.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/580713-language-tethers-us-to-the-world-without-it-we-spin">1251 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;We open our mouths and out flow words whose ancestries we do not even know. We are walking lexicons. In a single sentence of idle chatter we preserve Latin, Anglo-Saxon, Norse; we carry a museum inside our heads, each day we commemorate peoples of whom we have never heard. More than that, we speak volumes – our language is the language of everything we have read. Shakespeare and the Authorised Version surface in supermarkets, on buses, chatter on radio and television. I find this miraculous. I never cease to wonder at it. That words are more durable than anything, that they blow with the wind, hibernate and reawaken, shelter parasitic on the most unlikely hosts, survive and survive and survive.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/814477-we-open-our-mouths-and-out-flow-words-whose-ancestries">36 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right" href="/work/quotes/2877263-moon-tiger">More quotes&hellip;</a>
